HORACE  HARDEN  ALBRIGHT 
1976 


Since  1954  the  Regional  Oral  History  Office  has  been  interviewing 
leading  participants  in  or  well -placed  witnesses  to  major  events  in  the 
development  of  Northern  California,  the  West, and  the  Nation.   Oral  history  is 
a  modern  research  technique  involving  an  interviewee  and  an  informed 
interviewer  in  spontaneous  conversation.   The  taped  record  is  transcribed, 
lightly  edited  for  continuity  and  clarity,  and  reviewed  by  the  interviewee. 
The  resulting  manuscript  is  typed  in  final  form,  indexed,  bound  with 
photographs  and  illustrative  materials,  and  placed  in  The  Bancroft  Library  at 
the  University  of  California,  Berkeley,  and  other  research  collections  for 
scholarly  use.   Because  it  is  primary  material,  oral  history  is  not  intended 
to  present  the  final,  verified,  or  complete  narrative  of  events.   It  is  a 
spoken  account,  offered  by  the  interviewee  in  response  to  questioning,  and  as 
such  it  is  reflective,  partisan,  deeply  involved,  and  irreplaceable. 


************************************ 

All  uses  of  this  manuscript  are  covered  by  a  legal 
agreement  between  the  University  of  California  and 
Horace  M.  Albright  dated  February  8,  1986.  The 
manuscript  is  thereby  made  available  for  research 
purposes.   All  literary  rights  in  the  manuscript, 
including  the  right  to  publish,  are  reserved  to  The 
Bancroft  Library  of  the  University  of  California, 
Berkeley.   No  part  of  the  manuscript  may  be  quoted  for 
publication  without  the  written  permission  of  the 
Director  of  The  Bancroft  Library  of  the  University  of 
California,  Berkeley. 

Requests  for  permission  to  quote  for  publication 
should  be  addressed  to  the  Regional  Oral  History  Office, 
486  Library,  University  of  California,  Berkeley  94720, 
and  should  include  identification  of  the  specific 
passages  to  be  quoted,  anticipated  use  of  the  passages, 
and  identification  of  the  user. 

It  is  recommended  that  this  oral  history  be  cited  as 
follows: 

Horace  Albright,  "Mining  Lawyer  and 
Executive,  U.S.  Potash  Company,  U.S. 
Borax,  1933-1962,"  an  oral  history 
conducted  in  1986  by  Eleanor  Swent, 
Regional  Oral  History  Office,  The 
Bancroft  Library,  University  of 
California,  Berkeley,  1989. 


Copy  no. 


Catalogue  card 

Albright,  Horace    (1890-1987))  Mining  executive 

Mining  Lawyer  and  Executive.  U.  S.  Potash  Company.  U.S.  Borax. 
1933-1962  1989,  xvi,  117  pp. 

Recollections  of  early  California  mining;  class  of  1912,  University 
of  California;  mining  lawyers;  leaving  National  Park  Service  in 
1933  to  become  president  of  U.S.  Potash  Company;  potash  mining, 
Carlsbad,  New  Mexico;  lowering  workweek  to  fifty-two  hours;  hiring 
Hispanic  workers;  relations  with  mine  unions.  Contains  excerpts 
from  "Reminiscences  of  Horace  Albright,"  interview  done  for 
Columbia  University  Oral  History  Office,  1962. 

Introductions  by  Marian  Albright  Schenck  (daughter) ;  Amelia  Fry 
(director,  Earl  Warren  Project,  Regional  Oral  History  Office) ; 
James  Gerstley  (president,  retired,  U.S.  Borax  &  Chemical 
Corporation) . 

Interview  conducted  1986  by  Eleanor  Swent 

Western  Mining  in  the  Twentieth  Century  Oral  History  Series

PREFACE 


The  oral  history  series  on  Western  Mining  in  the  Twentieth  Century 
documents  the  lives  of  leaders  in  mining,  metallurgy,  geology,  education  in 
the  earth  and  materials  sciences,  mining  law,  and  the  pertinent  government 
bodies.   The  field  includes  metal,  non-metal,  and  industrial  minerals,  but  not 
petroleum. 

Mining  has  changed  greatly  in  this  century:   in  the  technology  and 
technical  education;  in  the  organization  of  corporations;  in  the  perception  of 
the  national  strategic  importance  of  minerals;  in  the  labor  movement;  and  in 
consideration  of  health  and  environmental  effects  of  mining. 

The  idea  of  an  oral  history  series  to  document  these  developments  in 
twentieth  century  mining  had  been  on  the  drawing  board  of  the  Regional  Oral 
History  Office  for  more  than  twenty  years.   The  project  finally  got  underway 
on  January  25,  1986,  when  Mrs.  Willa  Baum,  Mr.  and  Mrs.  Philip  Bradley, 
Professor  and  Mrs.  Douglas  Fuerstenau,  Mr.  and  Mrs.  Clifford  Heimbucher,  Mrs. 
Donald  McLaughlin,  and  Mr.  and  Mrs.  Langan  Swent  met  at  the  Swent  home  to  plan 
the  project,  and  Professor  Fuerstenau  agreed  to  serve  as  Principal 
Investigator. 

An  advisory  committee  was  selected  which  included  representatives  from 
the  materials  science  and  mineral  engineering  faculty  and  a  professor  of 
history  of  science  at  the  University  of  California  at  Berkeley;  a  professor 
emeritus  of  history  from  the  California  Institute  of  Technology;  and 
executives  of  mining  companies. 

We  note  with  much  regret  the  death  of  two  members  of  the  original 
advisory  committee,  both  of  whom  were  very  much  interested  in  the  project. 
Rodman  Paul,  Professor  Emeritus  of  History,  California  Institute  of 
Technology,  sent  a  hand-written  note  of  encouragement  just  a  few  weeks  before 
his  death  from  cancer.   Charles  Meyer,  Professor  Emeritus  of  Geology, 
University  of  California  at  Berkeley,  was  not  only  an  advisor  but  was  also  on 
the  list  of  people  to  be  interviewed,  because  of  the  significance  of  his 
recognition  of  the  importance  of  plate  tectonics  in  the  genesis  of  copper 
deposits.   His  death  in  1987  ended  both  roles. 

Thanks  are  due  to  other  members  of  the  advisory  committee  who  have 
helped  in  selecting  interviewees,  suggesting  research  topics,  and  raising 
funds . 

Unfortunately,  by  the  time  the  project  was  organized  several  of  the 
original  list  of  interviewees  were  no  longer  available  and  others  were  in 
failing  health;  therefore,  arrangements  for  interviews  were  begun  even  without 
established  funding. 


ii 


The  project  was  presented  to  the  San  Francisco  section  of  the  American 
Institute  of  Mining,  Metallurgical,  and  Petroleum  Engineers  (AIME)  on  "Old- 
timers  Night,"  March  10,  1986,  when  Philip  Read  Bradley,  Jr.  was  the  speaker. 
This  section  and  the  Southern  California  section  provided  initial  funding  and 
organizational  sponsorship. 

The  Northern  and  Southern  California  sections  of  the  Woman's  Auxiliary 
to  the  AIME  (WAAIME) ,  the  California  Mining  Association,  and  the  Mining  and 
Metallurgical  Society  of  America  (MMSA)  were  early  supporters.   Several  alumni 
of  the  University  of  California  College  of  Engineering  donated  in  response  to 
a  letter  from  Professor  James  Evans,  the  chairman  of  the  Department  of 
Materials  Science  and  Mineral  Engineering.   Other  individual  and  corporate 
donors  are  listed  in  the  volumes.   The  project  is  ongoing,  and  funds  continue 
to  be  sought. 

Some  members  of  the  AIME,  WAAIME,  and  MMSA  have  been  particularly 
helpful:  Ray  Beebe ,  Katherine  Bradley,  Henry  Colen,  Ward  Downey,  David 
Huggins,  John  Kiely,  Noel  Kirshenbaum,  and  Cole  McFarland. 

The  first  five  interviewees  were  all  born  in  1904  or  earlier.   Horace 
Albright,  mining  lawyer  and  president  of  U.S.  Borax  and  Chemical  Corporation, 
was  ninety-six  years  old  when  interviewed.   Although  brief,  this  interview 
will  add  another  dimension  to  the  many  publications  about  a  man  known 
primarily  as  a  conservationist. 

James  Boyd  was  director  of  the  industry  division  of  the  military 
government  of  Germany  after  World  War  II,  director  of  the  U.S.  Bureau  of 
Mines,  dean  of  the  Colorado  School  of  Mines,  vice  president  of  Kennecott 
Copper  Corporation,  president  of  Copper  Range,  and  executive  director  of  the 
National  Commission  on  Materials  Policy.   He  had  reviewed  the  transcript  of 
his  lengthy  oral  history  just  before  his  death  in  November,  1987. 

Philip  Bradley,  Jr.,  mining  engineer,  was  a  member  of  the  California 
Mining  Board  for  thirty- two  years,  most  of  them  as  chairman.   He  also  founded 
the  parent  organization  of  the  California  Mining  Association,  as  well  as  the 
Western  Governors  Mining  Advisory  Council. 

Frank  McQuiston,  metallurgist,  vice  president  of  Newmont  Mining 
Corporation,  died  before  his  oral  history  was  complete;  thirteen  hours  of 
taped  interviews  with  him  were  supplemented  by  three  hours  with  his  friend  and 
associate,  Robert  Shoemaker. 

Gordon  Oakeshott,  geologist,  was  president  of  the  National  Association 
of  Geology  Teachers  and  chief  of  the  California  Division  of  Mines  and  Geology. 

These  oral  histories  establish  the  framework  for  the  series;  subsequent 
oral  histories  amplify  the  basic  themes. 


ill 


Future  researchers  will  turn  to  these  oral  histories  to  learn  how 
decisions  were  made  which  led  to  changes  in  mining  engineering  education, 
corporate  structures,  and  technology,  as  well  as  public  policy  regarding 
minerals.   In  addition,  the  interviews  stimulate  the  deposit,  by  interviewees 
and  others ,  of  a  number  of  documents ,  photographs ,  memoirs ,  and  other 
materials  related  to  twentieth  century  mining  in  the  West.   This  collection  is 
being  added  to  The  Bancroft  Library's  extensive  holdings. 

The  Regional  Oral  History  Office  is  under  the  direction  of  Willa  Baum, 
division  head,  and  under  the  administrative  direction  of  James  D.  Hart, 
director  of  The  Bancroft  Library. 

Interviews  were  conducted  by  Malca  Chall  and  Eleanor  Swent. 

Willa  K.  Baum,  Division  Head 
Regional  Oral  History  Office 

Eleanor  Swent,  Project  Director

INTRODUCTION  by  Amelia  R.  Fry 


Because  the  processing  of  this,  his  final  Regional  Oral  History  Office 
[ROHO]  transcript,  coincides  with  the  end  of  the  life  of  Horace  Harden 
Albright,  a  farewell  more  than  an  introduction  is  in  order,  partly  for  Horace 
and  partly  on  behalf  of  his  partnership  with  ROHO,  which  he  made  bloom 
steadily  through  the  years.   It  should  be  said  that  the  interview  transcribed 
in  this  volume  is  different  for  another  reason:   it  sidesteps  Horace's  role  as 
"Mr.  Conservation"  (as  Fortune  Magazine  once  named  him)  to  capture  him  as  a 
businessman  leading  the  U.S.  Borax  and  Chemical  Corporation. 

He  was  born  in  1890,  which  means  that  by  the  time  historians  were 
discovering  the  marvels  of  electronically  recorded  memoirs,  as  well  as  the 
growing  field  of  resource  conservation  history,  he  was  just  the  right  age  for 
being  interviewed.   He  had  behind  him  twenty  years  of  leadership  in  the 
National  Park  Service,  then  several  decades  more  as  an  outside  catalyst  for 
saving  this  earth  and  the  life  on  it.   It  is  small  wonder  that  interviewers 
sought  out  this  heroic  figure  more  often  than  almost  any  other  person  in  the 
United  States.   The  open,  friendly  man  from  Inyo  County,  California,  seemed  to 
have  been  born  for  oral  history.   He  had  an  uncanny  memory  for  names  and  the 
pertinent  anecdote  as  well  as  the  ability  to  reel  out  candid  narratives  of 
complex  controversies.   With  extraordinary  accuracy  he  could  recall  in  amazing 
detail  how  it  came  about  that  some  of  the  most  wondrous  features  on  the  face 
of  this  nation  are  still  as  beautiful  as  when  she  was  young- -places  like  the 
Grand  Canyon  and  the  Grand  Tetons,  to  name  'just  two. 

He  was  called  on  to  contribute  to  the  Colonial  Williamsburg  Oral  History 
Collection- -one  of  the  first  studies  of  an  event  done  by  taping  the 
participants  of,  in  this  case,  the  acquisition  and  preservation  of  an  historic 
community.   Columbia  University  also  recorded  a  large  autobiographical 
interview  with  him,  as  have  many  presidential  libraries  and  conservation 
history  projects.   And  so  it  went,  through  the  sixties  and  seventies  and  into 
the  eighties. 

In  1961  the  Regional  Oral  History  Office  also  "discovered"  this  alumnus 
of  the  Berkeley  campus.   Today  in  The  Bancroft  Library  his  name  shines  forth 
from  the  royal  blue  spines  of  several  ROHO  oral  histories,  beginning  with  one 
in  which  he  and  another  National  Park  director,  his  1912  classmate  Newton 
Drury,  exchanged  views  about  the  national  parks  historic  issues;  they  recorded 
that  one  jointly  the  day  Horace  was  on  the  Berkeley  campus  to  receive  an  LL.D. 
Over  the  years  he  also  contributed  an  anecdotal  interview  about  his  classmate, 
California  Governor  and  Chief  Justice  Earl  Warren;  another  about  UC  Berkeley 
President  Gordon  Sproul ;  in  addition,  The  Bancroft  Library  was  able  to  barter 
a  copy  of  Newton  Drury 's  ROHO  oral  history  in  exchange  for  the  autobiography 
that  Horace  had  taped  with  the  Oral  History  Research  Office  at  Columbia 
University. 

But  it  is  his  behind-the-scenes  assistance  that  stands  the  tallest.   It 
began  when  we  discussed  an  idea- -a  wish- -to  produce  more  systematic  oral 
histories  by  doing  a  series  from  several  angles  on  one  subject,  documenting 
forest  policy  history  with  retired  top  executives  of  the  U.S.  Forest  Service. 


vii 


The  most  compelling  fact,  however,  was  that  such  a  series  would  require 
supplemental  funding,  and  for  that  Horace  turned  into  consultant  on  the  spot, 
guiding  us  along  the  unaccustomed  trail  of  supplication  and  justification,  and 
putting  us  in  touch  with  Resources  for  the  Future,  Inc.   in  Washington,  D.C., 
which  he  had  chaired.   When  the  incredible  day  arrived  with  ROHO's  very  first 
grant  in  its  mail  basket,  Albright  was  as  jubilant  as  we. 

At  that  point  he  became  a  comrade-in-arms,  and  we  gained  insight  into  the 
strength  of  his  sense  that  what  is  right  is  right:  he  had  encouraged  the  whole 
process  even  though  it  concerned  another  land  agency,  one  that  was  responsible 
for  resource  use  rather  then  preservation  and  as  such  often  had  been  his  rival 
when  acquiring  western  lands  for  parks. 

Horace  also  helped  with  numerous  small  undertakings  with  phone  calls  and 
letters,  such  as  introducing  us  to  a  fellow  member  of  his  remarkable  Class  of 
1912  whom  we  considered  fair  game  for  interviewing,  or  guiding  us  to  other 
pioneer  officials  of  the  Park  Service  whose  interviews  could  complement  those 
ROHO  had.   He  even  authored  introductions  to  two  interviews.   The  biggest 
challenge  of  all  took  shape  as  a  shared  but  nebulous  vision  of  an  enormous 
series  of  interviews,  a  project  that  would  cover  many  fields  and  decades 
documenting  Chief  Justice  Earl  Warren's  life  during  his  years  as  district 
attorney,  attorney  general,  and  governor,  as  well  as  the  operations  of 
political  parties  and  the  state  government. 

Albright  agreed  that  this  was  crucial  for  the  historiography  of  the 
period,  but  ROHO  could  not  proceed  until  we  had  the  chief  justice's  agreement 
to  cooperate.   Arranging  a  chance  to  talk  with  him- -he  was  then  still  on  the 
bench- -was  difficult,  even  with  the  help  of  the  president  of  the  university 
during  Warren's  brief  visits  to  California.   We  did  not  compete  well  for  his 
time  with  his  children  and  grandchildren. 

Then  Horace  apprised  us  that  that  very  year,  1962,  was  the  year  of  the 
fiftieth  reunion  of  the  Class  of  1912,  and  Earl  Warren  himself  was  planning  to 
attend.   Here  Horace  and  his  wife,  Grace  (also  a  classmate),  carried  off  a 
scenario  for  which  we  forever  lament  the  absence  of  a  video  camera.   On  the 
big  day,  the  ROHO  offices  turned  into  Communications  Center,  with  hopeful 
interviewers  hovering  around  the  telephones  while  downstairs  on  Ground  Zero 
(The  Bancroft  Library's  elegant  main  rooms),  well-dressed  septuagenarians 
greeted  each  other  at  the  refreshment  tables.   Horace  stationed  himself  at  the 
door  waiting,  of  course,  for  Warren  to  walk  through  it. 

Grace  Albright  was  not  among  the  guests,  not  yet.   She  was  in  charge  of 
surveillance,  and  as  the  chief  justice  progressed  peripatetically  across 
campus,  Grace  repeatedly  called  in  his  position  to  ROHO.   Finally,  she  phoned 
cryptically  that  he  was  heading  for  the  Bancroft;  then  she  hurried  to  the 
library  as  ROHO  also  evacuated  to  the  punch  bowl  downstairs. 

The  plan  worked,  of  course.   Grace,  only  slightly  out  of  breath,  greeted 
the  unsuspecting  Earl  Warren  joyously,  and  Horace  made  introductions  to  his 
ROHO  co-conspirators,  who,  nervous  in  their  boldness,  managed  to  explain  their 
hopes  that  the  chief  justice  would  tape-record  his  memoirs.   Yes,  Horace  put 
in,  telling  "Earl"  he  ought  to  get  busy  on  such  a  project;  he  owed  it  to  his 


viii 


alma  mater  and  adding  (to  our  delight)  that  he  knew  ROHO  always  did  a  good 
job.   Warren  was  smiling  and  agreeable,  and  invited  correspondence  to  spell 
out  the  plan.   We  left  feeling  ten  feet  tall,  but  that  was  not  the  end  of  it 
for  Horace.   He  knew  that  nailing  down  a  specific  date  with  the  chief  justice 
would  be  even  more  difficult.   And  he  never  let  the  matter  drop  whenever  he 
was  writing  or  talking  to  his  classmate. 

For  instance,  the  next  fall,  in  the  unlikely  location  of  the  Island  of 
Rhodes,  the  Earl  Warrens  happened  to  be  cruising  the  Mediterranean  with  the 
Drew  Pearsons  and  Katherine  Graham.   As  Horace  was  hiking  down  the  dry 
mountainside  from  the  Acropolis,  his  unbelieving  eyes  targeted  Earl  Warren  in 
a  group  of  people  in  the  distance.   After  the  elation  at  such  a  coincidental 
meeting,  Horace  got  right  to  the  point,  reminding  Warren  that  he  had  promised 
to  do  an  oral  history  project  and  reiterating  that  he  owed  it  to  his  alma 
mater. 

When  the  National  Endowment  for  the  Humanities  offered  ROHO  a  grant,  but 
with  the  proviso  that  it  must  be  matched  with  donations  post  haste,  Horace 
helped  supply  names  of  persons  to  solicit.   Once  the  project  began,  he  was  one 
who  suggested  persons  to  interview  whom  he  knew  had  had  long  years  of 
association  with  Earl  Warren. 

He  did  this  even  though  to  his  distress  the  student  sit-ins  and  quasi- 
military  actions  were  besmirching  the  good  name  of  the  campus  he  so  revered. 
In  the  beginning,  much  student  opposition  was  fueled  by  the  very  1964 
Goldwater  presidential  campaign  that  Horace,  a  Republican,  was  working  for-- 
al though  his  G.O.P.  was  closer  to  the  Earl  Warren  variety  and  he  could  not 
agree  with  all  of  Goldwater 's  stands.   [For  instance,  one  day  he  explained  to 
this  writer  his  conviction  that  Goldwater 's  statements  about  dropping  an  atom 
bomb  on  Vietnam  were  dictated  only  for  party  unity  with  its  conservatives  and 
should  not  be  taken  seriously.   His  interpretation  of  Goldwater 's  statement 
was  shared  with  this  author  during  a  visit  in  the  Grand  Tetons  in  the  summer 
of  1964.]   Horace  felt  that  any  student  lucky  enough  to  get  a  start  in  his  or 
her  life's  work  at  the  University  of  California  (which  then  charged  no 
tuition)  was  obligated- -as  he  also  had  reminded  Earl  Warren- -to  do  honor  to 
that  institution. 

He  seemed  to  feel  this  on  both  moral  and  practical  levels.   One  day  when 
visiting  ROHO  he  shook  his  head  in  disbelief  at  the  mess  left  behind  after  an 
outdoor  sit-in  against  the  war.   In  a  speech  in  1968  he  took  the  occasion  to 
insert  the  brief  reminder  that  "...students  in  our  day  did  not  engage  in 
rioting  or  otherwise  disturb  the  tranquility  of  the  university."   [The  speech 
is  an  address  he  delivered  June  16, -1968,  at  a  dinner  in  Eureka,  California, 
commemorating  the  fiftieth  anniversary  of  the  founding  of  the  Save  the 
Redwoods  Leaque.]   But  even  though  loyalty  was  out  of  fashion,  Horace's  own 
commitment  remained  unshaken  both  to  the  University  and  to  our  office.   In  his 
nineties  and  battling  against  arthritis  and  bad  vision  as  he  typed  on  his  old 
portable  typewriter,  he  still  worked  to  help  ROHO  with  its  difficulties  in 
launching  some  interviews  with  leaders  in  mining. 

We  developed  deep  respect  and  love  for  this  man,  for  the  same  reasons 
people  everywhere  have.  Whether  in  the  wildlife  preserves  of  Africa  or  the 


viii 


alma  mater  and  adding  (to  our  delight)  that  he  knew  ROHO  always  did  a  good 
job.   Warren  was  smiling  and  agreeable,  and  invited  correspondence  to  spell 
out  the  plan.   We  left  feeling  ten  feet  tall,  but  that  was  not  the  end  of  it 
for  Horace.   He  knew  that  nailing  down  a  specific  date  with  the  chief  justice 
would  be  even  more  difficult.   And  he  never  let  the  matter  drop  whenever  he 
was  writing  or  talking  to  his  classmate. 

For  instance,  the  next  fall,  in  the  unlikely  location  of  the  Island  of 
Rhodes,  the  Earl  Warrens  happened  to  be  cruising  the  Mediterranean  with  the 
Drew  Pearsons  and  Katherine  Graham.   As  Horace  was  hiking  down  the  dry 
mountainside  from  the  Acropolis,  his  unbelieving  eyes  targeted  Earl  Warren  in 
a  group  of  people  in  the  distance.   After  the  elation  at  such  a  coincidental 
meeting,  Horace  got  right  to  the  point,  reminding  Warren  that  he  had  promised 
to  do  an  oral  history  project  and  reiterating  that  he  owed  it  to  his  alma 
mater. 

When  the  National  Endowment  for  the  Humanities  offered  ROHO  a  grant,  but 
with  the  proviso  that  it  must  be  matched  with  donations  post  haste,  Horace 
helped  supply  names  of  persons  to  solicit.   Once  the  project  began,  he  was  one 
who  suggested  persons  to  interview  whom  he  knew  had  had  long  years  of 
association  with  Earl  Warren. 

He  did  this  even  though  to  his  distress  the  student  sit-ins  and  quasi- 
military  actions  were  besmirching  the  good  name  of  the  campus  he  so  revered. 
In  the  beginning,  much  student  opposition  was  fueled  by  the  very  1964 
Goldwater  presidential  campaign  that  Horace,  a  Republican,  was  working  for-- 
although  his  G.O.P.  was  closer  to  the  Earl  Warren  variety  and  he  could  not 
agree  with  all  of  Goldwater 's  stands.   [For  instance,  one  day  he  explained  to 
this  writer  his  conviction  that  Goldwater 's  statements  about  dropping  an  atom 
bomb  on  Vietnam  were  dictated  only  for  party  unity  with  its  conservatives  and 
should  not  be  taken  seriously.   His  interpretation  of  Goldwater 's  statement 
was  shared  with  this  author  during  a  visit  in  the  Grand  Tetons  in  the  summer 
of  1964.]   Horace  felt  that  any  student  lucky  enough  to  get  a  start  in  his  or 
her  life's  work  at  the  University  of  California  (which  then  charged  no 
tuition)  was  obligated- -as  he  also  had  reminded  Earl  Warren- -to  do  honor  to 
that  institution. 

He  seemed  to  feel  this  on  both  moral  and  practical  levels.   One  day  when 
visiting  ROHO  he  shook  his  head  in  disbelief  at  the  mess  left  behind  after  an 
outdoor  sit-in  against  the  war.   In  a  speech  in  1968  he  took  the  occasion  to 
insert  the  brief  reminder  that  "...students  in  our  day  did  not  engage  in 
rioting  or  otherwise  disturb  the  tranquility  of  the  university."   [The  speech 
is  an  address  he  delivered  June  16, -1968,  at  a  dinner  in  Eureka,  California, 
commemorating  the  fiftieth  anniversary  of  the  founding  of  the  Save  the 
Redwoods  Leaque.j   But  even  though  loyalty  was  out  of  fashion,  Horace's  own 
commitment  remained  unshaken  both  to  the  University  and  to  our  office.   In  his 
nineties  and  battling  against  arthritis  and  bad  vision  as  he  typed  on  his  old 
portable  typewriter,  he  still  worked  to  help  ROHO  with  its  difficulties  in 
launching  some  interviews  with  leaders  in  mining. 

We  developed  deep  respect  and  love  for  this  man,  for  the  same  reasons 
people  everywhere  have.  Whether  in  the  wildlife  preserves  of  Africa  or  the 


ix 


board  rooms  at  Rockefeller  Center  or  the  crowded  offices  of  ROHO,  he  was 
intelligent  enough  to  cut  through  to  underlying  problems,  and  he  always  cared 
enough  to  help.   Horace  would  never  have  thought  of  himself  as  a  teacher; 
however,  he  did  teach  people.   He  was  a  walking,  breathing  model  of  the 
confidence  which  lent  enthusiasm  to  his  work  once  he  believed  a  goal  was 
justified.   To  us,  he  carried  an  exposed  wisdom  of  how  one  accepts 
frustrations  with  the  pace  of  bureaucratic  procedure  without  losing  a  sincere 
respect  for  the  institution.   He  seemed  to  revive  a  tired  truism  with  his  Inyo 
County  "pioneer  individualism"  that  triggered  his  inner  warning  button  when  a 
line  of  limitations  had  been  breached,  when  it  was  time  to  say,  "Enough."  And 
he  taught  us  how  to  keep  in  mind  the  next  step . 

Horace's  own  next  step  he  once  outlined  to  this  writer  so  that  she  could 
help  in  approaching  the  National  Geographic  for  a  public  presentation.   The 
idea  of  it  was  born  when  he  opened  a  1966,  all-California  issue  of  the 
National  Geographic  and  saw  that  the  eastern  slope  of  the  Sierras,  his  back 
yard  so  strung  with  natural  jewels,  had  been  treated  rather  lightly.   In  his 
direct  way,  he  simply  told  his  longtime  friend  Melville  Grosvenor  of  the 
National  Geographic  about  the  error  in  judgment,  but  that  was  not  the  end. 

The  more  Horace  thought  about  it,  the  more  concretely  a  plan  formed,  one 
of  stunning  dimensions  for  the  National  Park  system  and  for  the  valley  walled 
by  the  highest  mountain  ranges  in  the  contiguous  states,  Owens  Valley.   The 
child  Horace  had  watched  its  farms  reduced  to  dust  when  its  river's  waters 
were  diverted  to  the  City  of  Los  Angeles. 

"It  was  a  lovely  valley  to  me  in  my  childhood,"  said  Horace  in  1966. 
"Now  it  has  no  future  except  recreation."   It  was  time  to  give  it  that  raison 
d'etre,  Horace  said.   Take  the  entire  stretch  of  incredible  California 
landscape  east  of  the  Sierras  from  Death  Valley  up  through  Alpine  County  and 
Mono  Lake- -an  area  longer  than  the  Washington- Boston  corridor  but  with  a 
fraction  of  the  population- -and  make  it  into  one  huge,  grandly  diverse 
national  recreation  area. 

Ninety- five  percent  of  the  land  was  already  in  some  sort  of  government 
ownership,  mostly  federal,  Horace  pointed  out,  and  Death  Valley  National 
Monument  was  a  natural  nucleus.   He  among  all  people  knew  well  the  contention 
inherent  in  transferring  lands  from  one  federal  agency  to  another.   But  the 
variety!   Horace  described  how  people  hike  and  climb  among  mountain  ranges 
that  tower  well  over  10,000  feet  and  ski  most  of  the  months  of  the  year  in  the 
resorts.   They  fish  and  hunt  in  an  abundance  of  creeks  and  forested  moraines 
of  incredible  beauty.   They  swim  or  boat  the  year  around  in  the  lower,  warmer 
valleys.   The  ancient  Indian  sites  and  old  mining  ghost  towns  are  fascinating 
historic  areas,  and  the  diminishing  herd  of  tule  elk  roam  his  valley.   This 
vision  glowed  with  possibility  for  Horace.   It  could  be  a  playground  par 
excellence  for  America,  California's  ultimate  gift  to  the  nation. 

This  particular  "next  step"  of  Horace's  was  never  realized.   We  did 
present  an  article  plan,  but  the  decision  at  National  Geographic  carried  some 
irrefutable  logic;  namely  that  in  fairness  to  "other  parts  of  the  nation,"  the 
magazine  could  not  return  to  California  with  another  large  story  in  the  near 
future.   [Correspondence  between  National  Geographic  and  Amelia  Fry,  late 


1966,  and  notes  on  conversations  with  Horace  Albright,  1966,  in  possession 
of  this  author. ] 

But  he  did  succeed  with  another  "next  step,"  which  admittedly  he  had  been 
"resisting  for  fifty  years,"  and  he  accomplished  it  at  age  ninety- five.   With 
Robert  Cahn,  Horace  co-authored  his  final  book,  The  Birth  of  the  National  Park 
Service.   [Horace  Albright  as  told  to  Robert  Cahn,  The  Birth  of  the  National 
Park  Service:   The  Founding  Years.  1913-33.  (Salt  Lake  City:   Howe  Brothers, 
Institute  of  the  American  West  books,  v.2)  1985.]  Cahn  is  a  leading  writer  on 
conservation  and  as  patient  and  persistent  as  Albright.   Over  a  period  of  many 
years,  visit  by  visit,  Cahn  and  his  wife,  Pat,  tape-recorded  Albright's 
detailed  memory,  then  as  a  team  added  their  research  and  editorial  talents. 
It  is  a  cohesive  reconstitution  of  Horace's  ground- level  view  of  the  first 
twenty  years  of  the  National  Park  Service's  life,  truly  its  Albright  Years -- 
although  Horace  would  never  have  agreed  to  such  a  name. 

Like  that  era  of  the  national  parks,  ROHO's  crucial  first- growth  years 
will  always  rest  on  our  Albright  Years,  too,  those  very  critical  ones  from 
1961  to  his  death  in  1987. 

Amelia  R.  Fry 

Project  Director,  Earl  Warren  Project 

Regional  Oral  History  Office 

February  1989 
Washington,  D.C. 


xi 


INTRODUCTION  by  James  M.  Gerstley 


I  first  met  Horace  Albright  in  New  York  in  1933.  I  had  been  working  in  San 
Francisco  and  was  returning  to  England  with  my  parents,  who  had  been  visiting 
my  mother's  family  in  San  Francisco.  My  father,  James  Gerstley,  was  a  senior 
executive  with  Borax  Consolidated  Ltd.  of  London  and  invited  Horace  Albright  to 
dinner,  as  Horace  was  about  to  leave  his  post  as  director  of  the  National  Park 
Service  to  become  general  manager  of  United  States  Potash  Company,  a  company 
in  which  BCL  had  a  50  percent  interest. 

The  United  States  Potash  Company  had  been  formed  to  mine  and  process  the 
potassium  ore  which  had  been  discovered  near  Carlsbad,  New  Mexico,  by  accident 
by  the  firm  of  Snowden  &  McSweeny  while  drilling  for  oil.   The  American 
subsidary  of  BCL- -the  Pacific  Coast  Borax  Company- -had  heard  of  this 
discovery,  reported  it  to  BCL  who  offered  to  finance  and  operate  the  necessary 
plant  and,  as  a  result,  after  negotiations,  had  acquired  its  interest  in  USP. 
In  those  days  potash  lands  could  only  be  leased  from  the  U.S.  government  and 
it  was  felt  that  someone  with  proven  government  experience  should  be  one  of 
the  top  executives  of  the  company. 

Horace  Albright's  father- -George  Albright- -had  for  many  years  worked  in 
Candelaria,  Nevada,  as  a  carpenter  and  engineer  at  the  Northern  Belle  mine. 
There  he  had  met  and  become  friends  with  Christian  Brevoort  Zabriskie  and 
together  they  had  served  as  Candelaria' s  only  coffin-builders  and  undertakers 
under  the  name  "A  to  Z  Company."   Zabriskie  later  joined  the  Pacific  Coast 
Borax  Company  and  rose  to  become  its  general  manager,  with  R.C.  Baker- - 
managing  director  of  BCL- -as  president.   Horace  Albright,  after  joining  what 
was  then  called  the  Bureau  of  National  Parks,  went  to  Yellowstone  as 
superintendent  of  that  park  and  field  director  of  the  bureau  from  1919  to 
1929.   It  was  in  Yellowstone  that  he  first  met  Baker,  who  was  on  a  visit  with 
Zabriskie,  and  it  was  undoubtedly  Zabriskie,  who  had  known  the  Albright  family 
for  years,  who  introduced  Horace  to  Baker.   Baker  saw  Horace  on  later  business 
trips  and  it  is  not  surprising  that  BCL  turned  to  Horace  when  it  was  decided 
that  someone  familiar  with  government  operations  should  become  general  manager 
of  USP.   At  the  same  time,  since  Horace  was  a  staunch  Republican  and  had 
served  under  Coolidge  and  Hoover,  he  was  not  happy  when  Roosevelt  with  Ickes 
as  Secretary  of  the  Interior  came  into  office,  and  so  welcomed  the  opportunity 
of  leaving  Washington. 

I  joined  Pacific  Coast  Borax  Company  in  1933  at  its  headquarters  in  Los 
Angeles  after  Frank  Jenifer  had  succeeded  Zabriskie  as  general  manager. 
Jenifer  was  invited  to  become  a  member  of  the  board  of  USP.   Horace  became 
president  of  USP  when  McSweeney,  the  first  president,  died,  and  I  was  invited 
to  become  a  member  of  the  board  after  Jenifer  died  in  1950. 

A  New  Mexican  company  had  to  abide  by  the  rule  that  there  be  an  interval 
of  at  least  one  hour  after  the  nomination  of  directors  to  allow  the  counting 
of  ballots.   This  was  a  farce  in  our  case  as  we  had  only  four  stockholders, 
three  of  whom  were  on  the  board  and  the  fourth  was  BCL.   However,  we  had  to 
obey  the  law,  so  Horace  used  to  entertain  us  with  films  of  national  parks.   I 
can  only  recall  these  meetings  as  some  of  the  longest  and  most  boring  I  ever 


xii 


had  to  attend.   We  were  also  taken  on  almost  every  visit  to  see  the  famous 
Carlsbad  Caverns. 

While  Horace  was  not  a  businessman  of  wide  experience,  he  was  an  able 
administrator,  well  liked  by  his  staff  and  associates,  and  was  willing  to 
listen  to  people  with  more  business  or  technical  experience.   He  had  a  good 
sense  of  humor  and  was  invaluable  when  it  came  to  handling  governmental 
matters  of  which  there  were  many. 

In  1956  it  was  decided  that  the  Pacific  Coast  Borax  Company  should  become 
an  independent  American  company  and  that  it  should  take  over  the  United  States 
Potash  Company  by  merger.   As  a  result,  the  United  States  Borax  &  Chemical 
Corporation  came  into  existence  and  I  became  its  first  president  and  Horace 
became  one  of  my  directors.   He  and  his  wife,  Grace,  soon  afterwards  moved 
from  New  York  to  California.   Grace  died  in  1980  and  Horace  in  1987,  and  until 
his  death  his  mind  and  recollection  seemed  almost  as  good  as  when  he  was  much 
younger. 

James  M.  Gerstley 

President  (retired) 

U.S.  Borax  and  Chemical  Corporation 


April  1989 
Atherton,  California 


xiii 


INTRODUCTION  by  Marian  Albright  Schenck 


Horace  Albright  always  regarded  his  life's  work  and  accomplishments  as  a 
twist  of  fate.   For  the  first  twenty- four  years  of  his  life,  his  ambition  was 
to  be  involved  in  some  way  with  mining.   In  his  boyhood  years  it  was  to  follow 
in  the  footsteps  of  his  hero,  grandfather  Horace  Harden,  who  had  come  from 
Maine  to  California  via  the  Isthmus  of  Nicaragua  in  1851.   Of  course,  Marden's 
call  was  GOLD,  and,  until  he  was  nearly  seventy  years  of  age,  he  followed  the 
rise  and  fall  of  the  gold  and  then  the  silver  strikes- -Columbia,  Mokelumne 
Hill,  Aurora,  etc.   He  combined  mining  with  trucking  in  supplies  to  the  camps, 
managing  stamp  mills,  even  lumbering.   Albright's  father,  George,  was  also 
drawn  to  the  excitement  of  the  mines,  coming  from  his  native  Canada  in  1873  to 
work  as  a  millwright  in  Virginia  City.   Only  after  the  1890  birth  of  his 
eldest  son,  Horace,  did  he  settle  down  in  Bishop,  California  to  build  houses, 
operate  a  mill  and  even  become  the  town's  undertaker! 

So  young  Horace  Albright  grew  up  with  these  two  strong  men,  listening  to 
the  tales  of  the  Gold  Rush  days,  meeting  the  old-timers  who  came  to  visit. 
Mining  was  an  exciting,  living  thing!   But  there  was  one  great  difference.   He 
wanted  to  go  to  college,  become  a  lawyer,  a  mining  lawyer.   And  he  did  just 
that,  graduating  in  the  class  of  1912  from  the  University  of  California  at 
Berkeley  and  entering  its  law  school,  Boalt  Hall. 

Now  this  is  where  the  twist  of  fate  comes  in.   One  of  Horace's  law 
professors  was  Will  Colby,  and  he  not  only  whipped  up  Horace's  interest  in 
mining  law,  but  he  began  to  take  the  young  man  around  with  him  to  meetings  of 
the  Sierra  Club,  introducing  him  to  John  Muir  and  his  philosophy  and  finally 
swaying  him  to  accept  an  offer  of  Adolph  Miller,  a  fellow  professor  at 
Berkeley.   Miller,  who  had  been  appointed  Assistant  Secretary  of  the  Interior 
in  1913,  suggested  to  Albright  that  he  come  to  Washington  with  him,  to  accept 
a  position  as  private  secretary  to  Secretary  of  the  Interior  Franklin  K.  Lane. 
Horace  went  to  his  mentor,  Colby,  who  convinced  him  that  he  could  finish  law 
school  back  East,  get  vast  experience  in  governmental  affairs,  and  then  return 
after  a  year  or  two  and  join  his  law  firm  in  San  Francisco. 

And  everything  went  according  to  plan- -until  one  day  in  December  1914 
when  he  was  introduced  to  Stephen  T.  Mather.   Miller  had  left  the  Interior 
Department  to  help  set  up  the  Federal  Reserve  system.   Mather  had  replaced 
him.   And  Mather's  goals  were  set  on  doing  something  about  the  national  parks, 
which  were  orphans  adrift  in  the  Interior  Department.   Lane  suggested  that  the 
young  attorney  Albright  could  help  Mather- -for  one  year  only- -force  a  bill 
through  the  Congress  to  establish  a  National  Park  Service  bureau  to  administer 
these  federal  areas.   It  was  an  enormous  decision  to  be  made  by  young 
Albright- -but  the  power  of  Mather's  personality  overcame  his  inclination  to  go 
home  to  California,  the  dream  of  his  lifetime  to  be  involved  in  the 
fascinating  world  of  mining.   Instead  he  turned  his  future  toward  national 
parks,  conservation,  preservation  of  historic  sites.   Horace  Albright  became  a 
founder  with  Mather  of  the  National  Park  Service,  architect  for  parks  here  in 
America  and  around  the  world  and  the  man  who  brought  the  federal  government 
into  the  realm  of  historic  preservation.   But  here  is  the  twist  of  fate  once 
more . 


xiv 


Albright  was  director  of  the  National  Park  Service  when  he  left 
government  service  in  1933  to  become  the  president  of  United  States  Potash 
Company,  a  mining  company  of  New  Mexico.   Who  had  led  him  into  this  position? 
None  other  than  Chris  Zabriskie,  who  headed  a  sister  company  directed  by  the 
same  British  corporation.   And  who  was  Chris  Zabriskie?  He  had  been  a  partner 
of  George  Albright  back  in  the  1880s  in  Candelaria,  Nevada  days  when  they  had 
a  business --"A  TO  Z  WE  DO  EVERYTHING." 

During  his  business  career  and  to  the  end  of  his  long  life  at  ninety- 
seven  years,  Horace  Albright  was  honored  for  his  interest  in  mining  with 
various  university  degrees.   He  was  also  given  the  Medal  of  Freedom  by  the 
president  of  the  United  States,  the  highest  gift  a  grateful  nation  can  bestow 
on  a  civilian,  for  his  lifelong  dedication  to  preserving  America  much  as  he 
had  found  it  in  the  Sierra  mountains  of  California  in  1890. 

Marian  Albright  Schenck 


October  1988 

Studio  City,  California 


XV 


INTERVIEW  HISTORY 

Horace  Albright  was  selected  as  one  of  the  first  in  the  oral  history 
series  on  Western  Mining  in  the  Twentieth  Century;  he  represents  mining 
lawyers  and  executives.   He  also  encouraged  and  advised  on  the  series.   He  had 
long  been  a  friend  to  the  Regional  Oral  History  Office;  in  her  introduction  to 
him,  Amelia  Roberts  Fry  details  this  warm  relationship. 

She  interviewed  him  because  of  his  fame  as  a  conservationist.   [Horace 
Harden  Albright  and  Newton  Bishop  Drury,  Comments  on  Conservation.  1900  to 
1960.  an  interview  conducted  by  Amelia  Roberts  Fry,  1962.]   His  training, 
however,  was  in  mining  law  and  he  had  a  twenty-nine -year  career  as  a  mining 
executive . 

"I  was  educated  as  a  mining  lawyer,  and  you  get  mining  in  your  blood,"  he 
says.   "My  grandfather  and  father  were  mining  men. .. .wherever  I  went,  I  liked 
to  see  mines,  go  into  mines,  look  at  the  mines."   So  he  explains  his  departure 
from  the  directorship  of  the  National  Park  Service  in  1933  to  become  acting 
president,  and  later  president,  of  the  first  successful  potash  mining  venture 
in  the  Western  Hemisphere,  a  pioneer  in  mining  and  refining,  in  reducing  the 
work  week  from  fifty-six  hours,  and  in  non-discriminatory  labor  policies. 
James  Gerstley,  president  of  U.S.  Borax  and  Chemical  Corporation  when  Albright 
was  a  director,  introduces  him  in  this  role,  and  explains  the  successive 
changes  of  name  of  the  company,  from  U.S.  Potash  to  Pacific  Coast  Borax  to 
U.S.  Borax. 

Horace  Albright  had  a  birthright  connection  with  mining;  his  grandfathers 
Albright  and  Marden  both  came  to  the  California  gold  districts  in  1851.   His 
mother  was  born  at  Mokelumne  Hill;  his  father  worked  in  the  mines  at  Bishop, 
California,  and  Candelaria,  Nevada.   A  poignant  reminder  of  how  much  times 
have  changed  is  that  his  mother  was  one  of  three  surviving  from  a  family  of 
nine;  one  died  of  typhoid  fever  and  four  died  within  ten  days,  of  diphtheria. 
He  considers  his  grandfather's  success  in  terms  of  the  fine  grave  markers  he 
was  able  to  provide  for  his  family. 

After  graduating  in  mining  from  the  University  of  California  with  the 
celebrated  class  of  1912,  Horace  Albright  studied  mining  law  until  he  went  to 
Washington  to  work  on  legislation  which  resulted  in  the  establishment  of  the 
National  Park  Service. 

In  connection  with  the  twenty-ninth  Horace  Albright  Lecture  in 
Conservation  at  the  University  of  California  at  Berkeley  on  March  16,  1989, 
Henry  J.  Vaux  said,  "Horace  Albright  saw  no  philosophical  conflict  between 
mining  and  National  Parks,  provided  you  did  not  try  to  locate  them  both  in  the 
same  place.   He  had  a  profound  commitment  to  future  generations,  but  also 
perceived  that  that  commitment  could  not  be  met  without  also  meeting 
legitimate  needs  of  the  present.   This  humanistic  view  offered  a  framework 
which  could  accommodate  the  apparent  conflicts  between  the  two  phases  of  his 
career. " 

Horace  Albright  was  interviewed  on  February  8,  1986  in  a  convalescent 
home  in  Studio  City,  California.   There  was  distraction  from  fellow  patients 


xvi 


calling  for  medication  and  aides  bringing  water;  it  was  far  from  an  ideal 
situation  for  tape-recording.   I  suspect  that  I  was  more  nervous  about  his 
advanced  age  (ninety-six  years)  than  he  was.   He  was  completely  alert,  and 
except  for  dryness  of  the  mouth  had  no  difficult  with  the  mechanics  of  the 
interview.   He  was  working  on  a  book  based  on  his  correspondence  with  Harold 
Ickes,  former  secretary  of  the  interior,  and  had  books  and  papers  piled  neatly 
around  him  in  the  small  room. 

It  was  fortunate  that  my  husband  was  present  at  first,  because  Mr. 
Albright  seemed  uneasy  talking  about  mining  to  a  woman.   When  he  found  that  my 
husband  was  a  mining  engineer,  and  that  I  understood  something  of  the 
business,  he  talked  more  comfortably.   Much  has  been  said  about  the  amiability 
of  Horace  Albright,  and  I  regret  that  I  could  not  have  known  him  longer.   He 
was  extremely  gracious  to  me  and  to  the  orderly  and  aides  on  duty.   In  a 
situation  where  many  old  people  regret  their  loss  of  independence,  he  retained 
his  dignity. 

He  died  on  March  29,  1987,  and  did  not  review  the  transcript  of  this 
interview,  which  was  lightly  edited  for  clarity,  so  we  cannot  know  whether  he 
might  have  altered  any  of  it.   The  tapes  of  the  interview  are  deposited  in  The 
Bancroft  Library. 

The  bulk  of  his  papers  are  in  the  Department  of  Special  Collections  at 
the  University  of  California  at  Los  Angeles  .   The  University  of  California  at 
Berkeley  has  been  the  grateful  recipient  of  personal  memorabilia  from 
undergraduate  days,  including  Horace  Albright's  class  hats  and  Grace 
Albright's  dance  cards  from  courtship  days.   Marian  Albright  Schenk,  their 
daughter ,  has  donated  other  documents  which  are  in  the  mining  archive  of  The 
Bancroft  Library;  she  also  wrote  a  personal  introduction  for  this  volume  and 
contributed  the  memorial  from  a  Japanese  colleague  of  her  father's. 

We  thank  Dr.  Ronald  Grele,  director  of  the  Columbia  University  Oral 
History  Office,  for  permission  to  include  in  this  volume  a  pertinent  section 
from  The  Reminiscences  of  Horace  M.  Albright,  an  interview  conducted  in  1962 
by  William  Ingersoll. 

We  also  thank  Joe  Kern,  manager  of  corporate  archives,  U.S.  Borax,  for 
making  photographs  available,  as  well  as  for  contributing  other  materials  for 
the  mining  archive . 

Horace  Albright  has  been  the  subject  of  many  books,  articles,  films,  and 
interviews,  and  this  volume  is  presented  as  an  affectionate  final  tribute  to 
his  total  persona  as  a  pre-eminent  Californian. 

Eleanor  Swent 
Interviewer -Editor 


10  March  1989 

Regional  Oral  History  Office

University  of  California  at  Berkeley 


HORACE  ALBRIGHT 

Interview,  February  8,  1986 
Studio  City,  California 


Interviewer:   Eleanor  Swent 
Also  present:   Langan  Swent 


I   CHANGES  IN  MINING  LAW  AND  EDUCATION//// 


E.  Swent:   Mr.  Albright,  you  were  involved  for  a  long  time  with  the  American 
Mining  Congress . 

Albright:   Who's  the  head  of  that  now? 

L.  Swent:   John  Knebel  is  the  new  head. 

Albright:   He's  taken  the  place  that  Julian  Conover  used  to  have. 

L.  Swent:  Yes.  Julian  Conover  retired  as  executive  director  of  the  American 
Mining  Congress  many  years  ago,  and  then  Allen  Overton  took  Julian 
Conover 's  place,  and  Allen  Overton  is  up  for  retirement  this  year. 

Albright:   Is  that  so?  Just  the  one  fellow  in  between?   I  knew  Overton.   The 
United  States  Chamber  of  Commerce  had  a  man  named  Smith,  handled 
all  our  mining  needs  and  so  forth.   I  was  very  active  in  that  kind 
of  mining,  but  actually,  I  was  not  a  miner.   I  was  a  mining 
lawyer. 

L.  Swent:   There  are  a  lot  of  lawyers  who  got  to  the  top  of  mining  companies. 

Albright:   I  don't  know  who's  the  principal  mining  lawyer  now,  but  in  my  day 
there  were  just  two  or  three  of  them.   There  was  Costigan  over  in 
Denver,  and  Curtis  Lindley  and  Will  Colby  in  San  Francisco,  and 
Theodore  Martin  in  Los  Angeles.   Theodore  Martin  was  a  mining 
lawyer.   He  was  very  distinguished;  he  wrote  one  of  the  books  on 
mining  law.   Lindlev  on  Mines  was  the  principal  one;  that  was  the 
book  we  used  in  law  school . 

L.  Swent:   He  was  a  distant  relative  of  mine. 

Albright:   Oh,  was  he?  The  one  who  wrote  the  law  book?  Well,  he  was  the 

foremost  writer  on  mining  and  his  partner,  Will  Colby,  was  the  top 
man  in  the  Sierra  Club,  the  secretary  of  it  for  twenty  or  thirty 
years,  and  he  was  our  professor  at  the  University  of  California. 
He  was  my  law  professor,  teaching  mining  law  and  water  law. 


L.  Swent:   Water  law  is  getting  very  complex  now. 
Albright:   Well,  mines  are  on  the  way  out,  aren't  they? 

L.  Swent:   Mining  law,  as  far  as  claims  go,  and  the  conflict  of  claims  and 
titles  and  all  this,  is  not  a  very  big  business  nowadays. 

Albright:   No,  Kennecott's  on  the  way  out,  and  the  iron  mines--.   Even  potash 
is  going  out  of  the  United  States;  it's  mostly  in  Canada. 

L.  Swent:   Most  of  the  old  titles  have  gotten  settled,  so  most  of  the  mining 
law  now  is  basically  a  variety  of  business  law. 

Albright:   They  aren't  educating  mining  engineers  any  more,  are  they? 

They're  mineralogists  or  geologists  or  people  that  deal  with  a 
particular  mineral . 

L.  Swent:   The  educational  lines  are  much  more  specialized. 
Albright:   Well,  do  they  call  them  mining  engineers? 

L.  Swent:   Oh  yes,  they  still  graduate  mining  engineers  too.   But  there's  an 
awful  lot  of  knowledge  out  there  now. 

Albright:   You're  a  mining  engineer? 

L.  Swent:   Somewhat.   I'm  retired  as  an  employee  with  Homestake  Mining 

Company  and  I  have  a  consulting  agreement  with  them  whereby  they 
gave  me  a  retainer  and  if  I  work  more  hours  than  the  retainer,  I 
bill  them  for  the  difference.   They've  just  turned  me  loose  to 
work  with  the  Mining  Congress. 

Albright:   You  probably  know  more  about  it  than  I  do.   I  don't  see  why  your 
wife  doesn't  interview  you  instead  of  me. 

L.  Swent:   She  wants  to  do  that. 
E.  Swent:   I  will,  I  will. 

Albright:   I  got  a  letter  from  Willa  Baum  of  the  Oral  History  Office  up 

there,  that  they  were  going  to  undertake  a  study  of  mining,  and  I 
assumed  it  was  only  mining  in  relation  to  the  university.   The 
whole  industry,  well,  you've  got  a  hold  of  something  there. 

Of  course  the  university  benefited  from  mining,  but  they 
didn't  start  the  university  until  the  California  rush  was  over. 
In  1868,  it  was  pretty  well  gone,  the  old  mining  rush,  but  there 
were  mining  people  who  came  from  there,  and  of  course  the  Hearst 
was  a  mining  fortune.   People  think  of  Hearst  as  a  publishing 
fortune,  but  it's  a  mining  fortune.   The  big  center,  and  the

My  wife  went  to  Hearst  Kindergarten. 

Phoebe  Hearst  endowed  the  kindergarten  in  Lead,  South  Dakota,  that 
I  went  to . 

Oh,  you  were  in  Lead.   I've  been  through  the  mines  at  Lead.   Now 
they've  got  a  new  mining  property  up  in  California  here  they  named 
after  Don  McLaughlin.   Don  and  I  were  in  college  together.   I  was 
two  years  ahead  of  him.   He  just  died  recently.   But  he  was  head 
of  Homestake  for  a  while.

No,  I  think  you'd  do  a  better  job  of  this  than  I  do  because  I  was 
just  on  the  outskirts,  you  know.   I  just  gave  her  a  short 
background  of  my  interest  in  mines,  how  it  started.   My 
grandfather  came  across  the  Isthmus  of  Nicaragua  in  1851.   He 
missed  the  gold  rush  by  two  years.   Your  wife  will  pardon  me  if  I 
quote- -you  probably  know  this  as  well  as  I  do,  and  even  better; 
you  probably  know  more  of  it,  the  old  poem: 

The  miners  came  in  forty-nine, 
The  whores  in  fifty- one; 
And  when  they  got  together, 
They  had  a  Native  Son.   [laughter] 

There  was  a  long  poem  about  that,  but  that's  the  only  section  I 
can  remember.   It's  just  as  well  I'd  forgotten  that. 

Did  you  read  the  book  by  James  Holliday,  The  World  Rushed  In? 
It's  very  well  done. 

No,  I  haven't.   If  you'd  asked  me  what  book  you  should  read,  not 
knowing  about  this  one ,  I  would  have  talked  about  John  Hays 
Hammond's  book.   A  big  thick  book.   I  knew  him  in  his  retired 
years  in  Washington.   He  built  that  magnificent  home;  I  guess  it's 
the  French  Embassy. 


E.  Swent:   He  made  a  great  fortune  from  mining. 


Albright:

Whew!   Well,  many  of  them  made  fortunes.   The  Floods  and  the  Mills 
and  a  good  many  of  them  made  fortunes.   I  don't  think  my  own 
grandfather  did;  and  yet  on  the  other  hand,  he  sent  two  girls  to 
college  and  he's  got  marble  statues  for  the  children  up  in  the 
Aurora  cemetery.   My  grandfather  was  in  Aurora. 

I  don't  think  the  oral  history  group  wants  to  go  back  into  the 
gold  rush  history;  that's  been  pretty  well  documented. 


It  seems  to  me  so,  yes.

The  California  history  of  mining  has  not  been  documented  very  much 
since  1933,  and  there 've  been  lots  of  changes  since  1933.  This  is 
sort  of  the  area  that  they  want  to  concentrate  on. 

Well,  they  wouldn't  get  anything  out  of  me  on  that  because  I  was-- 
well,  I  was  in  the  American  Mining  Congress,  and  then  I  was 
president  of  the  United  States  Potash. 


II  U.S.  POTASH,  1933-1962 


E.  Swent:   When  did  you  leave  your  position  as  director  of  the  National  Park 
Service  to  go  with  U.S.  Potash? 

Albright:   In  1933.   Snowden  and  McSweeney  were  oil  exploration  people,  that 
discovered  this  potash  about  1925,  discovered  it  drilling  oil. 
Oil  people  didn't  know  anything  about  mining,  and  so  they  scouted 
around  and  tried  to  get  a  mining  company  to  undertake  this 
exploration  and  development.   They  took  it  up  with  Kennecott  and 
another  mining  company,  and  eventually  with  the  old  Pacific  Coast 
Borax  Company,  at  that  time  controlled  in  England.   They  were 
mining  in  Death  Valley  by  that  time;  they  hadn't  left  there  yet. 
They  had  tried  to  do  a  little  potash  in  the  first  World  War,  so 
they  knew  quite  a  bit  about  potash. 

They  studied  it,  and  their  mining  engineer,  Clarence  Rasor, 
recommended  that  they  undertake  the  development  of  that  mine . 
They  bought  half  the  common  stock  of  the  United  States  Potash 
Company,  which  the  oil  company  organized.   They  also  agreed  to 
undertake  the  financing  and  development  of  the  mine  and  the 
refinery. 


Left  to  right:   J.G.  Home,  Mine  Superintendent;   Mrs.  Gertrude 
Stiehler,  Assistant  Secretary;   Colwell  A.  Pierce,  General 
Superintendent;   Horace  Albright,  President  and  General  Manager; 
Paul  Speer,  Vice  President  and  General  Counsel.   Carlsbad,  New 
Mexico,  c.  1946. 


Photograph  courtesy  U.S.  Borax  Corporate  Archives 


"^^ 


Left  to  right:   (first  man  and  woman  are  unidentified)  :  Tom 
Cramer,  Vice  President  U.S.  Potash;   Rose  Smith,  wife  of  a  New 
Mexico  State  Legislator;   Horace  Albright,  President  U.S.  Potash; 
Mrs.  C.A.  Pierce;   John  E.  Miles,  Governor  of  New  Mexico;   Mrs. 
Tom  Cramer;   C.A.  Pierce,  Superintendent  U.S.  Potash.   Refinery 
of  U.S.  Potash  Company  in  Carlsbad,  New  Mexico,  1947. 

Photograph  coursey  of  Marion  Albright  Schenck 


Executive  Head  of  an  Independent  Company 


Albright:   Then  of  course  came  the  question,  who's  going  to  run  it?  And  the 
reason  why  they  picked  on  me  was --well,  they  never  told  me.   It 
just  so  happened  that  the  vice  president  in  charge  of  the  Borax 
Company  in  the  United  States,  Chris  Zabriskie,  was  a  close  friend 
of  my  father's  in  the  early  days  of  Candelaria,  Nevada.   I  had 
been  in  touch  with  him  all  the  time.   And  Mr.  Mather,  who  was 
first  head  of  the  Park  Service,  had  made  a  fortune  in  borax 
mining,  at  a  mine  near  Manchester.   You  see,  they  had  sold  because 
they  had  lost  the  ore. 

At  any  rate ,  some  of  these  borax  people  had  seen  my 
management  of  Yellowstone  Park.   They  also  knew  about  my  education 
and  my  many  years  in  the  Interior  Department,  my  background  in 
mining,  and  my  education  in  mining  law- -I  was  a  lawyer  by  that 
time- -and  my  knowledge  of  the  Interior  Department,  and  my 
connections  in  Washington.   Also  of  course  I  knew  New  Mexico  very 
well;  that's  where  the  discovery  was  made.   They  put  all  those 
things  together,  and  they  decided  if  I'd  take  the  position,  to 
offer  it  to  me.   And  they  did;  they  offered  it  to  me  every  year 
for  three  years  while  they  were  building  the  mine  and  so  forth. 

I  had  things  to  do  as  head  of  the  Park  Service ,  and  I 
couldn't  accept.   But  after  Roosevelt  was  elected  in  1932,  they 
thought  that  I  might  be  willing  to  leave  the  government  then. 
Well,  by  that  time,  I'd  accomplished  about  all  I  wanted  to 
accomplish  anyway.   Besides  that,  I  had  two  children  to  educate, 
and  salaries  for  the  government  were  horrible,  you  know,  so  after 
Roosevelt  was  elected,  I  said,  well,  I  have  two  or  three  things  to 
do,  I  don't  want  to  go  now,  but  if  you  want  to  hold  it  open  a 
little  longer,  I  might. 

Well,  they  held  it  open  another  year,  and  I  accomplished  what 
I  wanted  to  in  the  Roosevelt  administration.   They  asked  me  to 
stay,  so  my  severing  the  connection  was  purely  voluntary;  it  had 
nothing  to  do  with  politics.   I  resigned,  and  took  the  position. 

It  was  a  very  unusual  kind  of  position  because  I  was  to  be 
the  executive  head  of  an  entirely  independent  company.   It  didn't 
belong  to  Snowden- Me Sweeney,  and  didn't  belong  to  Pacific  Coast 
Borax.   It  belonged  to  both  of  them,  but  I  was  to  be  independent. 

The  president  of  Snowden-McSweeney,  a  man  named  Henry 
McSweeney,  was  a  very  wealthy  man  already.   He  had  been  an 
attorney  for  John  D.  Rockefeller  at  one  time.   He  wanted  to  stay 
president;  he  just  had  a  feeling  that  he  wanted  to  be  president. 
He  wasn't  going  to  get  any  pay;  he'd  have  to  pay  his  own  expenses; 
all  the  emoluments  of  the  presidency  were  mine.   They  all  tried  to 
get  him  to  take  the  chairmanship,  and  let  me  be  president,  but  no, 


' 


Albright:   he  liked  the  title  of  president.   He  said  I  could  be  chairman  if  I 
wanted  to.   I  said,  well,  if  I  can't  be  president,  I'd  rather  be 
vice  president  and  general  manager. 

L.  Swent:   Where  was  it  headquartered? 

Albright:   New  York.   That's  where  McSweeney  was,  so  was  Pacific  Coast  Borax, 
all  there.   But  I  was  out  in  New  Mexico  a  great  deal  of  the  time 
because  we  were  far  and  away  the  first  ones  in  there,  you  know. 
The  Potash  Company  of  America  came  in  about  1934,  the  others  came 
in  after  that.   But  we  were  the  first  ones  there. 

We  opened  up  the  potash  mine.   In  1946,  Mr.  McSweeney  died. 
He  was  about  eighty-five  years  old,  and  never  had  anything  to  do 
with  the  company  except  at  board  meetings .   And  he  was  not  very 
good  at  that  because  he  was  very  deaf,  he  couldn't  hear  what  went 
on,  and  he  just  didn't  know  much  about  it.   Of  course,  he  cashed 
in  on  the  dividend  all  right.   When  he  died,  they  made  me 
president,  and  I  was  president  for  the  last  ten  years. 

Then  in  1956,  we  merged  the  company  with  the  borax  company, 
and  changed  the  name  to  United  States  Borax  and  Chemical.   That's 
the  name  now.   They're  here;  their  headquarters  are  here.   In  the 
meantime,  they've  quit  mining  in  Death  Valley  and  they're  mining 
up  near  Boron,  the  big  open  pit  mine.   Just  after  that  I  was  hired 
as  a  consultant  and  a  director  of  U.S.  Borax  and  Chemical;  that's 
the  last  position  I  had.   I  retired  from  that  in  1962.   I  was 
seventy- two  years  old  then. 

Right  after  that,  they  hired  Ronald  Reagan  as  their 
television  announcer,  you  know.   He  announced  the  "Death  Valley 
Days"  on  the  television  for  two  or  three  years.   When  he  was 
nominated  for  governor,  the  conservationists  were  afraid  he 
wouldn't  be  a  good  park  man  or  a  good  forest  man  and  he'd  do 
things  contrary  to  conservation,  so  they  had  an  agreement  that 
somebody  had  to  talk  to  Reagan,  and  acquaint  him  with  the 
principles  of  conservation,  because  he  was  just  an  actor,  he 
wasn't  supposed  to  know  anything  about  conservation.   I  was 
selected  to  instruct  Reagan,  and  so  just  after  he  was  nominated  as 
governor,  I  had  nearly  all  afternoon  with  him.   I  supplied  him 
with  literature  and  with  a  discussion  of  all  the  problems  of 
conservation  that  I  could  think  of,  and  we  got  along  fine.   But  I 
don't  talk  about  being  Ronald  Reagan's  advisor  at  all.   He  was 
good  on  conservation,  and  he  did  all  right  as  president. 

E.  Swent:   Was  there  any  conflict  between  parks  and  mining  when  you  were  with 
the  Park  Service? 

Albright:   No,  the  only  time  we  ever  had  any  discussion  of  mining  was  with 
tungsten.   During  the  first  war,  and  again  in  the  second  war. 
There  was  a  little  tungsten  in  the  upper  reaches  of  Yosemite,  and

they  wanted  to  make  sure  that  if  they  ran  out  of  tungsten,  over

go  in  there,  but  they  never  did.   There  was  no  mining  in  the

never  had  any  problem  so  far  as  the  mines  were  concerned. 

And  of  course  when  you  were  in  the  mining  business,  you  didn't 
have  a  problem  with  conservationists . 

No,  but  the  conservationists  counted  on  me,  in  mining,  to 
influence  keeping  mining  out.  Which  I  did.   When  anybody  talked 
about  looking  for  mines  in  the  parks,  you  could  depend  on  me 
opposing  it.   But  I  can't  remember  of  any  serious  efforts  to  get 
in  there  in  the  mining.   There  never  was  mineral  in  the  parks  to 
any  extent,  so  far  as  I  know.   Of  course  we  had  a  long  fight  with 
Senator  Cameron  in  Grand  Canyon  where  he  tried  to  get  control  of 
the  Grand  Canyon  with  mining  claims .   We  had  several  years  of 
contest  with  him.   Had  him  up  to  the  Supreme  Court  twice.   But  he 
was  in  there  before  we  had  a  Park  Service. 

There's  a  uranium  mine  developed  in  the  fifties. 

Uranium,  yes,  uranium,  just  one  lode  development- -they  finally  got 
rid  of  that.   They  couldn't  buy  it,  but  they  ran  out  of  uranium  on 
their  claim,  so  the  way  the  Park  Service  got  control  of  it,  they 
gave  them  permission  to  mine  a  little  more  under  the  government 
land,  in  return  for  the  whole  property.   So  the  government  owns  it 
all  now;  there's  no  mining  anywhere  now.   Well,  he  knows  more 
about  mining  that  I  do.   The  best  I  can  do  for  you  I  think,  and 
for  your  off ice- -I'm  very  much  interested  in  that  office  there. 

Did  you  know  William  Penn  Mott,  who's  now  head  of  the  Park 
Service?

L.  Swent:   Let's  see.   You  started  out  in  New  Mexico  as  U.S.  Potash,  and  then 
merged  and  became  U.S.  Chemical  and  Borax.   And  then  that  merged

Albright:   United  States  Borax,  which  is  what  it  is  now.   U.S.  Borax  and 

Chemical.   Now  it's  a  part  of  Rio  Tinto,  a  big  conglomerate,  it's 
got  about  200  companies  in  it. 

L.  Swent:   Did  you  have  some  serious  problems  with  the  Internal  Revenue 
Service? 

Albright:   No,  they  were  indicted  before  I  was  with  them,  when  they  were 
still  independent.   They  were  indicted  for  anti- trust.   They 
proved  something  on  them  with  American  Potash  and  Chemical ,  up 
here  at  Trona,  and  they  had  to  sign  a  consent  decree,  but  they 
haven't  been  in  any  trouble  that  I  know  of. 

L.  Swent:   My  father  testified  as  an  expert  witness  for  a  potash  company  in  a 
tax  hearing  in  New  York. 

Albright:   Oh,  Potash  Company  had  some  trouble  on  depletion  I  think.   I 
remember  hiring  both  Don  McLaughlin  and  Will  Colby. 

L.  Swent:   I  think  that's  the  one. 

Albright:   But  that  was  way  back,  that  was  back  in  the  thirties. 

L.  Swent:   Yes.   I  remember  I  dropped  in  at  the  trial  one  day  when  I  was  on 
my  way  to  school,  and  I  graduated  in  1933. 

Albright:   Where 'd  you  graduate  from? 

L.  Swent:   Moses  Brown  School,  a  prep  school  in  Providence,  Rhode  Island.   On 
my  way  to  it  one  year,  I  stopped  in  New  York  and  listened  to  some 
of  the  testimony. 

Albright:   That  must  have  been  the  one;  it's  the  only  one  I  remember.
