I  don't  know  why  I  can't  think  of  that  name;  you  know,  it 
shows  I'm  getting  old.   Well,  let's  drop  that  one  for  a  moment. 

There  were  three  or  four  families  that  were  in  mining  and 
were  interested  in  the  Mining  Congress.   I  know  we  used  to  get 
together  and  discuss  our  families.   And  of  course  there  were  oil 
people.   Pauley,  Pauley  Pavilion  and  so  forth,  he  was  an  oil  man. 
I  don't  know  whether  Doheny  or  some  of  those  Standard  people  were 
in  oil  or  not,  but  I  think  oil  is  regarded  as  a  mining  operation, 
isn't  it? 

L.  Swent:   Well,  they're  in  the  AIME  [American  Institute  of  Mining, 
Metallurgical,  and  Petroleum  Engineers]  now. 

Albright:   They  have  their  own  organization,  don't  they? 

L.  Swent:   The  American  Petroleum  Institute.   But  now,  petroleum  engineers 
are  a  part  of  the  AIME. 

Albright:  I  used  to  belong  to  that  too.   Do  you  belong? 

L.  Swent:  Oh,  yes;  I  still  do. 

Albright:  I  took  the  magazine  for  years.   Do  you  know  Jim  Boyd? 

L.  Swent:  Oh  yes. 

Albright:   He  comes  in  here  occasionally.   He's  up  in  Carmel  now.   His  father 
was  chief  engineer  of  the  borax  company. 

L.  Swent:   Jim  was  born  in  Australia. 

Albright:   They  were  born  in  Australia,  yes.   They  had  a  great  joke  about  his 


10 


Albright:   father,  Julian  Boyd,  when  he  came  up.   He  was  a  mining  engineer, 
and  was  hired  as  chief  engineer  for  Pacific  Coast  Borax.   They 
introduced  him  around  as  Mr.  Byrd,  and  he  couldn't  say  "Byrd." 
He'd  say,  "The  name  is  not  Boyd;  it's  Boyd."  They  played  that 
joke  on  him  for  a  long  time. 

But  Jim  I  think  is  one  of  the  smartest  mining  men  I  ever 
knew.   Of  course  he  was  drafted  right  away  in  the  war,  made  a  full 
colonel.   His  job  was  to  procure  minerals.   When  the  war  was  over, 
they  took  him  to  Germany,  to  make  a  study  of  the  situation  of  the 
German  mines .   He  came  back  early  in  1946 ,  but  nobody  knew  he  was 
back.   He  came  back  to  the  School  of  Mines,  where  he  had  been,  in 
Golden,  Colorado. 

The  Mining  Congress  was  holding  its  convention  in  Denver,  and 
I  was  presiding  at  the  first  meeting,  a  luncheon,  in  the  Brown 
Palace,  and  sitting  up  at  the  table,  the  man  who  was  going  to 
speak  at  the  luncheon,  was  the  Secretary  of  the  Interior,  Julius 
Krug.   He  succeeded  Ickes  in  Roosevelt's  cabinet.   The  fellows 
were  coming  in  to  the  luncheon,  and  I  saw  coming  through  the  door, 
there  was  Jim  Boyd.   So  I  said  to  Krug,  sitting  next  to  me,  "Mr. 
Secretary,  you  know  Jim  Boyd,  don't  you?  You  must  have  known  him 
during  the  war." 

"Sure,"  he  said,  "he's  in  Germany." 

I  said,  "No,  he  isn't.   I  think  he  just  came  in  here.   I 
thought  he  was  in  Germany ,  too . " 

"My  gosh,"  he  said,  "if  he's  here,  I  want  to  see  him." 

So  when  I  took  over  at  the  end  of  the  meal ,  and  took  care  of 
the  introductions,  I  asked  if  Colonel  James  Boyd  was  there,  would 
he  please  come  up  to  the  head  table .   Well ,  he  promptly  came  up 
there.   Krug  was  tickled  to  death  to  see  him.   He  right  away  took 
him  back  to  Washington  as  an  assistant,  and  then  he  made  him 
director  of  the  Bureau  of  Mines .   That  was  all  in  1946 . 
Afterwards  he  went  out  with  Copper  Range,  and  he  stayed  there 
until  he  retired. 

His  first  wife,  the  mother  of  four  children,  four  sons,  died. 
They  retired  to  the  Watergate  apartment  in  Washington.   After  she 
died,  after  a  year  or  two,  he  married  again.   That's  when  he  moved 
to  Carmel;  he's  in  Carmel  now. 

L.  Swent:  We  saw  him  at  the  California  Mining  Association  in  Monterey.  Do 
you  have  any  suggestions  on  people  that  should  be  interviewed  in 
this  project? 

Albright:   I  would  certainly  interview  Boyd.   He's  had  all  the  honors  that 
can  come  to  a  mining  man,  and  he's  a  very  brilliant  fellow.   I 


11 


Albright:   think  he'd  enjoy  meeting  and  talking  with  you. 

Another  mining  family  is  Searls.   Fred  Searls  was  chief 
engineer  and  president  of  Newmont.   The  next  one,  in  my  class, 
became  a  rancher  up  around  Tracy.   So  far  as  I  know  he  was  always 
in  ranching.   Then  Carroll  was  attorney  for  Newmont,  in  New  York. 
A  very  wonderful  fellow.   I  know  he's  dead  now.   Bob  Searls  was  a 
mining  lawyer  in  San  Francisco.   The  youngest  one  is  a  doctor,  and 
I  think  he ' s  in  Eureka .   Fred  was  in  the  class  of  1909 ,  and  I 
don't  know  if  he's  alive. 

L.  Swent:   He  was  a  good  friend  of  my  dad's  and  I  think  he's  dead  now. 

Albright:   They  kept  the  old  homestead  in  Nevada  City,  and  they  kept  it 

always  available.   They  had  a  housekeeper,  and  any  time  one  of  the 
boys  wanted  to  go  there  and  take  a  vacation,  it  was  always  there. 
That  was  true  clear  up  until  the  '70s.   I  stopped  there  in  the 
early  '70s  and  it  was  still  running. 

I  think  that  there's  a  good  story  for  your  office  in  what  the 
mining  fortunes,  and  the  men  who  had  them,  what  they  did  for  the 
university.   The  university  didn't  start  until  1868.   Mainly  it 
was  the  Hearsts  of  course. 

Another  place  where  you  might  at  least  get  some  atmosphere- - 
do  you  know  about  E  Clampus  Vitus?  Oh,  gosh--E  Clampus  Vitus  was 
a  fun  society  back  in  the  early  days,  way  back,  about  in  the 
[18] 40s  and  '50s.   Nobody  knows  what  E  Clampus  Vitus  means.   I 
guess  it  was  defunct  for  about  forty  years,  but  along  about  in  the 
'20s,  it  was  dug  up  and  reorganized.   Dane  Coolidge  was  writing 
about  the  mines,  and  he  was  one  that  dug  it  up  again.   I  think 
it's  headquartered  in  Stockton.   Professor  Wood  was  writing  on  it. 
He's  the  fellow  that  developed  that  museum  of  mining  at  Murphy's. 
You  ought  to  go  up  there.   You'd  get  lots  about  mining  up  there. 
You  take  Murphy's  and  Sutter's  Fort  and  Nevada  City  and  Grass 
Valley,  I  think  they've  all  got  exhibits  on  mining.   And  possibly 
Sonora.   They're  all  still  maintaining  lots  of  interest  in  the  old 
days . 

E  Clampus  Vitus  gets  out  a  little  slick  magazine  of  four  to 
six  pages  every  two  or  three  months.   It  doesn't  make  much  sense, 
and  yet  there's  some  history  running  through  it,  and  if  you  once 
join  it,  you  just  can't  make  yourself  quit. 

They've  got  quite  a  big  chapter  here,  and  there's  a  chapter 
up  in  Bishop  where  I  was  born,  called  the  Slim  Princess  Chapter. 
That's  what  they  called  the  railroad  up  there,  the  Slim  Princess. 
But  I  think  the  main  office  is  still  in  Stockton. 

My  grandfather  belonged  to  it.   I  remember  he  used  to  tell 
stories  about  it.   Among  other  things,  he'd  tell  stories  of 


12 

Albright:   incidents,  things  that  happened  to  miners.   I  remember  one  story 
that's  typical  of  what  they  did.   The  Palace  Hotel  was  built,  it 
was  a  Sharon  proposition,  and  all  the  mining  people  that  went  to 
San  Francisco  stayed  at  the  Palace  Hotel.   It  was  something 
different  in  the  whole  West,  the  Palace  Hotel  was.   It  had  the 
appointments  of  the  East  that  nobody  out  here  knew  anything  about, 
you  know. 

Well,  on  this  occasion,  a  miner  got  enough  money  to  go  to  San 
Francisco,  and  he  went  to  the  Palace  Hotel,  of  course.   And  he 
went  down  to  the  dining  room  for  dinner.   He  was  given  a  table  for 
one  because  he  was  alone,  and  the  waiter  came  along  and  said, 
"Soup,  sir?"   "No,  I  don't  want  any  soup,"  he  said.   And  another 
waiter  came  along  and  said,  "Soup,  sir?"  Well,  half  a  dozen 
waiters  came  and  asked  him  if  he  wanted  soup,  and  he  didn't  want 
it.   Finally  he  managed  to  stop  a  waiter  and  get  his  order  in,  and 
he  had  his  dinner. 

Well,  he  went  upstairs  after  dinner,  and  his  room  was  awfully 
hot.   He  hadn't  had  any  experience  with  heat.   He  didn't  know 
where  the  heat  was  coming  from,  and  he  didn't  know  how  to  shut  it 
off  if  he  had.   So  he  took  his  clothes  off  and  pitched  himself  on 
the  bed.   Well,  there  was  a  sick  man  in  the  room  next  to  him, 
which  he  didn't  know  about,  of  course,  and  the  nurse  came  up  to 
give  him  an  enema.   And  she  got  into  the  wrong  room  and  found  him 
lying  on  the  bed  and  she  gave  him  an  enema.   The  miner  said,  "I 
knew  they'd  get  the  damned  soup  into  me  one  way  or  the  other." 

[laughter]   That's  typical  of  the  stories  that  were  handed 
down.   And  they're  still  doing  it,  still  digging  up  those  old 
things.   That's  E  Clampus  Vitus.   I'll  see  if  I  can  send  you  a 
copy. 

The  retired  president  of  U.S.  Borax  is  living  in  Woodside. 
He's  active  with  the  Bancroft,  I  think,  Jim  Gerstley.   I  think  his 
children  went  to  Stanford.   But  I  keep  telling  Willa  [Baura] , 
everybody  I  know  is  dead.   When  you  get  to  be  ninety -six  all  my 
friends  are  dead. 

L.  Swent:   It  works  the  other  way  around  too.   It  reminds  me  of  the  minister 
at  church  who  was  telling  the  congregation  that  they  should 
forgive  their  enemies  and  turn  the  other  cheek,  that  you  should 
really  not  have  any  enemies.   Finally  he  said,  "Now,  tell  me,  is 
there  anyone  in  the  congregation  who  has  no  enemies?  Please  hold 
up  your  hand . " 

This  old  fellow  held  up  his  hand,  and  the  minister  said,  "You 
have  no  enemies?"  and  this  fellow  said,  "No."  The  minister  said, 
"Wonderful!   You  tell  the  congregation  how  you've  lived  in  order 
to  accomplish  that."  And  this  fellow  said,  "The  bastards  are  all 
dead."   [laughter]   So  that's  the  position  you're  in. 


13 


Albright:   My  enemies  are  all  dead,  and  as  my  daughter  pointed  out,  you  can 
call  them  anything  you  want  to,  it  doesn't  make  any  difference. 
You  can  say  anything  you  want  about  them.   It's  that  way  with  me. 


The  University  of  California  at  Berkeley. 

Albright:   I  was  at  Berkeley  from  1908  to  1913,  and  in  those  days,  the  mining 
department  was  quite  an  important  one.   They  were  turning  them 
out,  mining  engineers.   Quite  a  number  of  my  class  were,  but  what 
became  of  them  I  don't  know.   I  don't  remember  that  they  actually 
practiced  mining. 

I  studied  mining  law,  just  mining  cases  which  I  did  a  little 
bit.   I  only  did  the  one  year.   But  one  of  the  inducements  to  get 
me  to  go  to  Washington  was  that  they  made  mining  law  there,  and 
land  law  and  water  law.   It  was  a  good  place  to  be;  that's  how  I 
came  to  go  to  Washington.   I  didn't  know  anything  about  parks;  I'd 
never  been  in  one.   The  job  was  to  create  a  bureau.   First  thing  I 
knew,  I  was  in  it  pretty  deep,  and  one  thing  and  another  came 
along,  so  it  was  too  late  to  practice. 

My  class  of  1912  was  a  great  class .   Earl  Warren  was  in  our 
class,  you  know.   Five  of  us  have  been  made  alumni  of  the  year. 
One  of  the  officers  of  the  alumni  association  recently  said  that 
he  thought  the  class  of  1912  was  the  most  interesting  class  they'd 
ever  had.   Many  of  the  people  attained  distinction  of  one  kind  or 
other. 

Three  of  us,  including  myself,  have  been  head  of  the  Park 
Service .   Newton  Drury  was  head  of  the  Save  the  Redwoods  League . 
Of  course  Earl  Warren  was  our  greatest  one.   We  had  a  congressman 
from  Stockton  for  a  long  time,  named  Johnson.   Herman  Phleger,  a 
very  prominent  man,  was  one  of  the  lawyers  that  prosecuted  the 
Nazi  criminals  at  Nuremberg.   He  was  also  at  the  United  Nations. 
But  they're  practically  all  gone;  just  one  or  two  left  now.   The 
Phleger  boys  were  from  Stockton;  both  of  them  were  football 
players . 

We  even  had  distinction  in  athletics.   Stanford  had  been 
beating  California,  or  tying  them,  in  football,  for  six  or  seven 
years.   In  1908,  Stanford  won  again,  but  they  never  won  anything 
while  the  1912  people  were  there.   For  three  years,  California 
won,  and  also  the  graduate  year,  and  if  it  hadn't  been  for  the 
freshman  eligibility  rule,  we'd  have  won  the  first  year.   It  was 
the  same  way  with  other  athletics;  they  seemed  to  be  taking 
everything.   But  they're  all  gone  now,  all  gone. 

Transcriber:   Eleanor  Swent 
Final  typist:   Sophia  Hayes 


Seated:   Horace  Harden  Albright,  recipient  of  the 
U.S.  Medal  of  Freedom.   Standing:   Mary  Gower 
Albright  (widow  of  Albright's  son),  Roswell  Schenck, 
and  Marion  Albright  Schenck  (daughter) .   8  December 
1980.

Albright:     Hia  name  was  Christian  Brevoort.     He' a  long  sine*

Ky   dear  Willa: 

Thank  yfcu  for  your  letter  with  details  of  your  mining  history 
project.        It  IB  most   interesting.       I'm  glad  the  project  only  embraces 
California  mining  history,     prob  bly  with  emphasis  on    the  period 
1955  to  the  present.     Still  the  project  ie  rn  .numental  in  several  ways, 
and  expensive. 

Ycu  have  done  a  good  job  in  collecting  people  with  California 
mining  background  to  enlist  in  your  interviews,       I'm  sorry  I  cannot 
be  azor.g  them.       My  old  age  and  health  would  not  permit  my  active 
involvement,  but  while  I  headed  a  mining  company  for  25  years,  I  was  a 
chief  executive  officer,  the  top  administrator,   but  not  involved  in 
actual  mining  operations  which  were  the  responsibilities  of  mining  en 
gineers   and   other  technicians. 

The   same  is  trie  of  Jim  Gerstley.       For  a  few  years  he  was  Presi 
dent    of  the  U.S.  Borax  Ie,  Cheaical  Corp.   with  headquarters  here  in  Los

sources  to  be  mined  or  of  the  mining  industry.       B.C.L.  ha-   a  controlling 
interest  in  borax  production.     Jim  came  to  California,   and  for  awhile  worked 
for  the  Dow  Chemical  Corp.,  then^$i>ned  U.S.  Borax  as  an  administrative 
assistant.     Thus  wss  before  the  nerger  of  the  old  Pacific  Coast  Borax  Co 
and  my  U.S.  Potash  -  aughst  1956.     The  borax  mines   of  the  Death  Valley 
area  had  been  closed,   and  the  big  open  pit  mine  opened  at  Boron  north  of 
LOE  Angeles  near  the  EdwHards  Air  Force  Base.       Jim  Gerstley  had  nothir.g  to 
do  with  either  underground  mining   of  borax  in  Death  Valley  or  open  pit 
mining  where  borax   is  being  mined  today.

UNITED  PRESS  WTERNATONAL 

WASHINGTON  —  Horace  Mar- 
den  Albright,  a  co-founder  of  the 
National  Park  Service  and  a  long 
time  conservationist,  died  early  Sat 
urday  in  Los  Angeles.  He  was  97. 

National  Park  Service  spokes 
man  Duncan  Morrow  said  Mr.  Al 
bright  died  of  heart  failure  at  2:30 
a.m.  at  the  Chandler  Convalescent 
Home. 

Mr.  Albright,  who  was  awarded 
the  Medal  of  Freedom,  the  nation's 
highest  civilian  honor,  by  President 
Reagan  in  1981,  is  credited  with 
playing  a  major  role  in  the  legisla 
tion  establishing  the  National  Park 
Service  in  1916. 

The  park  service  maintains  and 
manages  the  nation's  79.5  million 
square  acres  of  national  lands. 

Mr.  Albright  was  working  in  the 
Interior  Department  in  the  admin- 
istration  of  President  Woodrow 
Wilson  when  he  and  his  close 
friend,  Stephen  T.  Mather,  drafted 
the  measure  creating  the  park  ser 
vice. 

When  Congress  passed  the  bill, 
Mr.  Albright  became  one  of  the  ser 
vice's  five  original  employees.  He 
was  later  appointed  as  the  first  park 
service  superintendent  of  Yellow 
stone  National  Park  and,  in  1929,  he 
succeeded  Mather  as  director  of  the 
park  service. 

He  left  the  service  in  1933  to  Join 
the  American  Potash  Co.,  but  he 
maintained  a  close  association  with 
the  agency  and  with  the  VS.  con 
servation  movement  throughout 
his  life.  He  retired  as  president  of 
American  Potash  in  1958. 

Born  in  Bishop,  Inyo  County,  on 
Jan.  6, 1890,  Mr.  Albright  graduated 
from  UC-Berkeley  before  going 
East  in  1913  to  work  with  Mather  in 
creating  the  park  service. 

He  is  the  author  of  several  books 
on  the  park  service,  the  most  recent 
of  which  —  "The  Birth  of  the  Na 
tional  Park  Service  The  Founding 
Years,  1913-1933"  —  was  published 
in  1986  and  co-written  by  Pulitier 
Prize-winning  author  Robert  Cahn 


He  is  survived  by  his  daughter, 
Marian  Shenck,  of  Studio  City,  with 
whom  he  lived  much  of  the  time  in 
recent  years;  daughter-in-law  Mary 
Albright  of  Irvine,  three  grand 
daughters;  a  grandson;  and  nine 
great-grandchildren. 


.5 


Horace  M.  Albright  12  was  fond  of 
pointing  out  that  he  and  Yosemite  Na 
tional  Park  were  born  the  same  year, 
1890.  Although  their  closeness  remained  a 
constant,  the  nature  of  Albright's  relationship 
to  the  park  changed  to  that  of  a  parent,  dur 
ing  the  time  he  served  as  director  of  the  Na 
tional  Park  Service,  from  1929  to  1933.  His 
concern  for  his  "child"  went  undiminished 
throughout  his  life,  and  it  is  certain  that  Al 
bright,  who  died  in  Los  Angeles  March  28  at 
the  age  of  97,  was  gratified  to  know  that 
Yosemite  would  survive  him. 

In  retrospect  the  relationship  seems  al 
most  fated.  Albright  was  born  in  California's 
high  country,  in  the  town  of  Bishop  in  the 
austerely  beautiful  White  Mountains.  The 
rugged  mountains  and  their  delicate  ecology 
taught  him  both  a  love  of  and  respect  for  nat 
ural  resources.  Being  a  member  of  Berkeley's 
distinguished  Class  of  1912 — Earl  Warren  and 
fellow  conservationist  Newton  B.  Drury 
were  classmates  and  friends — provided  early 
lessons  in  the  value  and  power  of  human  re 
sources. 

Albright  went  on  to  earn  a  law  degree  at 
Georgetown  University  in  1914  and  was  ad 
mitted  to  the  bar  in  both  California  and  the 
District  of  Columbia.  While  earning  his  law 


degree,  he  became  a  member  of  the  staff  of 
Franklin  Lane,  then  secretary  of  the  Depart 
ment  of  Interior  under  President  Woodrow 
Wilson.  As  the  department's  assistant  at 
torney  in  charge  of  national  park  affairs,  Al 
bright,  with  his  good  friend  Stephen  T. 
Mather,  was  instrumental  in  drafting  and  se 
curing  passage  of  the  legislation  establishing 
the  National  Park  Service  in  1916.  He  subse 
quently  served  as  the  service's  assistant  di 
rector,  from  1917  to  1919,  and  was 
superintendent  of  Yellowstone  National  Park 
from  1919  to  1929,  when  he  succeeded  Math 
er  as  park  service  director.  He  also  helped 
create  the  National  Conference  of  State 
Parks,  which  encouraged  the  establishment 
of  state  park  systems. 

Albright's  close  involvement  with  the  park 
service  from  its  inception  spawned  a  number 
of  historical  documents  on  the  enterprise.  He 
wrote  several  books  on  the  subject,  the  most 
recent  of  which,  in  1986.  was  The  Birth  of 
the  National  Park  Service:  The  Founding 
Years.  /°/j-/9jj,  co-written  with  the 
Pulitzer  Prize-winning  author  Robert  Cahn. 
He  also  collaborated  with  Drury  on  inter 
view  projects  for  Berkeley's  Regional  Oral 
History  Office  ( ROHO )  and  wrote  introduc 
tions  to  several  ROHO  histories  of  the  park 


service  and  related  American  conservation 
movements. 

After  his  term  as  director  of  the  National 
Park  Service,  Albright  began  a  career  associa 
tion  with  the  United  States  Potash  Company, 
beginning  as  vice  president  in  1933  and  re 
tiring  as  president  in  1956.  One  of  the  na 
tion's  foremost  conservationists,  Albright 
held  leadership  positions  in  a  myriad  of  con 
servation  ogranizations.  from  the  Save-the- 
Redwoods  League  to  the  Theodore  Roosevelt 
Association.  Other  important  affiliations 
were  with  the  American  Society  of  Land 
scape  Architects,  the  American  Scenic  and 
Historic  Preservation  Committee,  Resources 
for  the  Future,  and  the  Pacific  Tropical  Botan 
ical  Garden.  His  various  efforts  were  recog 
nized  with  a  series  of  major  awards, 
including  the  Theodore  Roosevelt  Associa 
tion  Distinguished  Service  Medal  ( 1959 ),  the 
American  Forestry  Association  Distinguished 
Service  Award  ( 1968 ),  the  Audubon  Medal 
( 1969 ),  and  the  Cosmos  Award  ( 1974 ).  In 
1980  President  Jimmy  Carter  bestowed  the 
Medal  of  Freedom  Award  ( 1981 ).  the  nation's 
highest  civilian  honor,  on  Albright,  calling 
him  "a  living  monument  like  the  Grand 
Tetons  he  has  fought  so  hard  to  preserve" 
and  "a  driving  force  for  conservation  in  this 
country'  during  most  of  the  20th  century." 

In  addition  to  the  Medal  of  Freedom,  the 
honors  of  which  Albright  was  most  proud 
were  the  state's  dedication,  in  1981.  of  a  ten- 
acre  tract  of  redwoods  in  the  South  Calaveras 
State  Park  as  "The  Horace  and  Grace  Albright 
Grove"  (  named  as  well  for  his  wife,  also  a 
member  of  Berkeley's  Class  of  '12 )  and  the 
naming  of  the  Horace  M.  Albright  Training 
Center,  in  Grand  Canyon  National  Park,  the 
major  school  for  the  training  of  National  Park 
rangers. 

Albright  also  maintained  strong  ties  to  the 
University  throughout  his  life.  He  received 
an  honorary  law  degree  from  UC  in  1961  and 
was  named  a  Berkeley  Fellow  in  1968.  He 
was  the  Alumni  Association's  Alumnus  of  the 
Year  for  1952.  one  of  five  members  of  his 
class  to  be  given  the  Alumni  Association's 
highest  honor.  An  endowed  lectureship  in 
his  name  in  Berkeley's  College  of  Natural  Re 
sources  has  served  as  a  platform  for  many  of 
America's  top  environmentalists.  In  1983  Al 
bright's  fraternity,  Del  Rev,  established  the 
Horace  Albright-Del  Rev  Scholarship  Award 
Program  to  aid  outstanding  students  in  vari 
ous  conservation  disciplines. 

Horace  Albright's  wife  of  65  years  died  in 
1980.  He  is  survived  by  his  daughter.  Marian 
Shenck.  of  Studio  City:  his  daughter-in-law. 
Mary  Albright,  of  Irvine;  three  grand- 
daughers;  a  grandson;  nine  great-grand 
children;  Yosemile  and  Yellowstone  national 
parks;  and  a  total  of  "9.5  million  acres  of 
public  land  protected  by  the  agency  he 
helped  create. 


California  Monthly 
June  1987 


3  (M987 


Dear  Friends": 

Every  year  from  1916  to  1979  Grace  and  Horace  Albright  sent  messages  at  Christmas 
to  their  friends.  Then  it  was  Just  Horace  alone  through  1986.  And  now  this  letter 
comes  to  you  early  this  year,  in  the  springtime,  because  there  will  be  no  Christmas 
message  for  1987- 

As  their  daughter,  but  speaking  for  my  husband,  my  brother's  wife,  Mary,  four 
grandchildren  and  their  spouses,  and  eleven  great-grandchildren,  I  feel  they  would 
want  me  to  tell  you  the  final,  story  and  say  goodbye  for  them. 

My  beloved  Mama  and  Papa  died  almost  seven  years  apart,  she  on  June  15,  1980  and 
he  on  March  28,  1987 •  The  sorrow  of  our  family  in  losing  these  extraordinary  people 
is  mitigated  by  the  surety  that  nothing  -  even  death  -  could  separate  them.  They 
loved  each  other  so  completely. 

To  all  of  you  who  receive  this,  it  is  a  certainty  that  you  were  touched  by  the 
intelligence,  humor,  zest  for  living,  warmth  *nd  friendship  they  both  radiated  as 
they  walked  through  life.  They  always  gave  so  much  to  their  family  and  friends  and 
received  such  happiness  in  return. 

When  she  left  him,  some  of  the  light  went  out  of  him.  He  was  90  years  old  and, 
shortly  thereafter,  near  death  himself.  Though  ever  regaining  his  physical  health, 
he  gathered  his  strength  of  spirit,  putting  his  ever  brilliant  intelligence,  memory 
and  sense  of  humor  to  wonderful  use  until  the  end.  Two.books  were  published,  "The 
Birth  of  the  National  Park  Service"  in  1985  and  r2ie  National  Park  Service:  The 
;Story  Behind  the  Scenery"  in  1987  as  well  as  several  magazines  articles  along  with 
more  to  appear  shortly.  As  I  left  him  on  Friday,  March  27th,  he  called  to  me, "Girlie, 
I'm  sure  I'll  feel  better  this  afternoon  and  I'll  get  on  with  the  Ickes  thing."  (He'd 
already  written  five  out  of  six  episodes  of  his  trips  with  Harold  Ickes,  Secretary 
of  the  Interior,'  in  1933)- 

Papa  received  almost  every  honor  a  man  could  and  appreciated  them  all,  but  never 
felt  the  Joy  in  them  that  he  derived  from  the  addition  of  a  hard-fought  piece  of 
land  for  Kings  Canyon,  a  battle  won  in  the  fight  for  the  Tetons  and  his  beloved  Jack 
son  Hole  or  the  experience  of  savoring  a  sublime  moment  in  one  of  his  treasured  parks. 
After  his  death  I  was  trying  to  complete  an  article  for  a  magazine  concerning  his 
setting  up  of  Hawaii  National  Park  in  1920.  I  was  putting  on  the  computer  his  vivid 
description  of  the  sunrise  in  Haleakala,  dictated  to  me  only  a  few  weeks  ago.  He 
said,  "It  was  a  fantastic  sight,  another  memory,  every  detail  of  which  I  recall  these 
67  years  later."  It  suddenly  struck  me  that  the  moment  he  was  describing  was  March  28, 
1920,  exactly  67  years  to  the  day  he  died,  but  his  freshness  of  vision  and  vibrancy 
of  life  was  with  him  still. 

On  behalf  of  our  family,  we  thank  each  of  you  for  the  love  and  friendship  you  have 
given  to  Grace  and  Horace  Albright  and,  through  them,  to  us.  We  hope  in  the  years  to 
come  we  may  keep  the  threads  of  their  ties  with  you. 

Faithfully  and  fondly,

CALIFORNIA  WILDLAND  CONSERVATION: 
UNITIES  AND  CONFLICTS' 

By 
Henry  J.  Vaui 

The  first  permanent  policy  for  conservation  of  forest  and  rangelands 
in  California  was  adopted  in  1 890,  the  year  Horace  Albright  was  born.  As  we 
approach  the  hundredth  anniversary  of  those  two  events  it  is  appropriate  to 
look  bade  over  the  first  Century  of  Conservation  in  our  State  to  see  what  it 
may  tell  us  about  the  process  by  which  conservation  policy  has  evolved  from 
the  simple  times  of  a  hundred  years  ago  to  the  very  complei  ones  which 
conservationists  must  face  today. 

William  Howard  Taft,  President  of  the  U.S.  during  the  years  that 
Horace  Albright  was  a  mining  engineering  student  here  at  Berkeley,  once 
said:  "A  great  many  people  are  in  favor  of  conservation  regardless  of  what  it 
means".  Although  I  suspect  that  Taft  was  giving  vent  to  a  bit  of  political 
cynicism  when  he  made  the  remark,  his  words  properly  evoke  both  the  wide 
diversity  of  concerns  that  conservationists  have  embraced,  then  as  now,. and 
the  power  of  the  word  conservation  as  a  political  symbol. 

As  to  the  diversity  within  conservation,  the  career  of  Horace  Albright 
himself  illustrates  it  beautifully.  From  1915  to  1933  he  worked  in  the 
Department  of  Interior,  first  to  establish  and  make  strong  the  National  Park 
Service  under  Stephen  T.  Mather,  later  to  eipand  the  National  Park  System 
as  Director  of  the  Service.  In  1933  he  left  the  government  to  become  an 
executive  for  the  United  States  Potash  Corporation,  a  mining  enterprise  with 
which  he  remained  until  retirement  in  1956.  After  that  he  was  Chairman  of 
the  Board  of  Resources  for  the  Future,  Inc.,  a  non-profit  foundation 
conducting  a  variety  of  research  into  topics  affecting  the  issues  which  its 
name  embraces. 

Horace  Albright  saw  no  philosophical  conflict  between  muling  and 
National  Parks,  provided  you  did  not  try  locate  them  both  in  the  same  place. 
He  had  a  profound  commitment  to  future  generations,  but  also  perceived 
that  that  commitment  could  not  be  met  without  also  meeting  legitimate 
needs  of  the  present.  This  humanistic  view  offered  a  framework  which  could 
accommodate  the  apparent  conflicts  between  the  two  phases  of  his  career.  It 
is  in  this  sense  that  I  will  suggest  that  Horace  Albright's  professional  life 
offers  us  a  paradigm  for  conservation  worth  reemphasizing  today. 


*  Prepared  for  the  29th  Horace  M.  Albright  Lecture  in  Conservation  at  the  University  of 
California .  Berkeley.  March  16. 1989. 


Eleanor  Herz  Swent 

Born  in  Lead,  South  Dakota,  where  her  father  became 
chief  metallurgist  for  the  Homestake  Mining  Company. 
Her  mother  was  a  high  school  geology  teacher  before 
marriage. 

Attended  schools  in  Lead,  South  Dakota.  Dana  Hall 
School,  and  Wellesley  College,  Wellesley,  Massa 
chusetts.   Phi  Beta  Kappa.  M.A.  in  English, 
University  of  Denver.   Assistant  to  the  President, 
Elmira  College,  New  York.  Married  to  Langan  Waterman 
Swent,  mining  engineer. 

Since  marriage  has  lived  in  Tayoltita,  Durango,  Mexico; 
Lead,  South  Dakota;   Grants,  New  Mexico;   Piedmont, 
California. 

Teacher  of  English  as  a  Second  Language  to  adults 
in  the  Oakland,  California  public  schools.  Author 
of  an  independent  oral  history  project,  Newcomers 
to  the  East  Bay,  interviews  with  Asian  refugees  and 
immigrants.   Oral  historian  for  the  Oakland 
Neighborhood  History  Project. 

Interviewer,  Regional  Oral  History  Office  since  1985, 
specializing  in  mining  history.
